    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";

          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };

          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer14967035727323097014">Alain de Botton's <i>The Consolations of Philosophy</i> takes the discipline of logic and the mind back to its roots. Drawing inspiration from six of the finest minds in history - Socrates, Epicurus, Seneca, Montaigne, Schopenhauer and Nietzsche - he addresses lack of money, the pain of love, inadequacy, anxiety and conformity. De Botton's book led one critic to call philosophy '</span>
  <span id="freeText14967035727323097014" style="display:none">Alain de Botton's <i>The Consolations of Philosophy</i> takes the discipline of logic and the mind back to its roots. Drawing inspiration from six of the finest minds in history - Socrates, Epicurus, Seneca, Montaigne, Schopenhauer and Nietzsche - he addresses lack of money, the pain of love, inadequacy, anxiety and conformity. De Botton's book led one critic to call philosophy 'the new rock and roll'.</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=writing">writing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=refrence">refrence</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer412675379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15338791475543470882">The Consolations of Philosophy, Alain de Botton<br/><br/>The Consolations of Philosophy is a nonfiction book by Alain de Botton. First published by Hamish Hamilton in 2000. <br/><br/>The title of the book is a reference to Boethius's magnum opus Consolation of Philosophy, in which philosophy appears as an allegorical figure to Boethius to console him in the year he was imprisoned, leading up to his impending execution. <br/><br/>In Consolations, de Botton attempts to console the reader through everyday problems (or at least </span>
  <span id="freeText15338791475543470882" style="display:none">The Consolations of Philosophy, Alain de Botton<br /><br />The Consolations of Philosophy is a nonfiction book by Alain de Botton. First published by Hamish Hamilton in 2000. <br /><br />The title of the book is a reference to Boethius's magnum opus Consolation of Philosophy, in which philosophy appears as an allegorical figure to Boethius to console him in the year he was imprisoned, leading up to his impending execution. <br /><br />In Consolations, de Botton attempts to console the reader through everyday problems (or at least help them to understand them) by extensively quoting and interpreting a number of philosophers. These are categorized in a number of chapters with one philosopher used in each.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز چهارم ماه نوامبر سال 2016میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: تسلی‌ بخشی‌ های فلسفه؛ نویسنده: آلن دو باتن؛ مترجم: عرفان ثابتی؛ تهران، ققنوس، 1383؛ در 303ص، نمودار، مصور؛ شابک 9643114791؛ چاپ سوم 1385؛ چاپ چهارم 1386؛ چاپ پنجم 1388، شابک 9789643114794؛ چاپ نهم 1392؛ موضوع: مشاوره فلسفی - سده 21م<br /><br />همیشه بهترینهایم را، برای انتهای کار نگاه میدارم، اینبار کمی هم تنبلی ذهنی بوده باشد شاید، که چند سال این کتاب را در انتظار نگاه داشته ام، در همین هفته ها بود، که دیدم همه میخوانند، و مینگارند، و انگار کردم، یک کتاب همچون «دنیای سوفی»، «یوستین گردر» دوباره پیدا شده لابد؛ که آوایش چنین دست به دست میگردد.؛ انگارم درست بود.؛ نخست از ترجمه ی بسیار خوب، و گوارای جناب ثابتی، باید بنویسم؛ آنگاه از نویسنده و نگارنده ی کتاب، که گزینش و آرایشش، سخت خوانی فلسفه ی «سقراط» بیست و شش سده پیش از امروز؛ «اپیکور» بیست و چهار سده پیش، «لوسیوس سنکا»ی «رمی»، در بیست سده ی پیش از امروز، «میشل دو مونتنی» سده ی شانزدهم میلادی، و «شوپنهاور» سده ی نوزدهم میلادی را، به مشاوره ای گوارا و امروزین بدل کرده است<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 30/05/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_241882440" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/241882440" />
    <a title="Riku Sayuj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1651956-riku-sayuj"><img alt="Riku Sayuj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433433464p2/1651956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/241882440?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Riku Sayuj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Riku Sayuj" href="/user/show/1651956-riku-sayuj">Riku Sayuj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140276619" href="/book/show/23423.The_Consolations_of_Philosophy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1651956-riku-sayuj?shelf=r-r-rs">r-r-rs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1651956-riku-sayuj?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1651956-riku-sayuj?shelf=pop-phil">pop-phil</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer241882440" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6765138731382794253"><br/>As I went through the book I was unable to make up my mind whether it was a work on philosophy masquerading as a self-help book to reach a wide audience or if it was a pretentious self-help book with aspirations to be a book on deep philosophy.<br/><br/>Even after I finished it, I am not sure how to judge it. Should I judge it harshly for picking and choosing among the works of these great philosophers to fit them into the narrow framework that Botton has drawn for them and thus making them draw his yoke?</span>
  <span id="freeText6765138731382794253" style="display:none"><br />As I went through the book I was unable to make up my mind whether it was a work on philosophy masquerading as a self-help book to reach a wide audience or if it was a pretentious self-help book with aspirations to be a book on deep philosophy.<br /><br />Even after I finished it, I am not sure how to judge it. Should I judge it harshly for picking and choosing among the works of these great philosophers to fit them into the narrow framework that Botton has drawn for them and thus making them draw his yoke? Or should I be lenient that Botton makes lofty thoughts so accessible by dragging them down and tethering them to the normal privations of men and offering consolations for the same?<br /><br />The title is of course a brilliant one and almost irresistible. Not original maybe, as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5589950.Boethius" title="Boethius" rel="nofollow noopener">Boethius</a> has already used it, but Boethius' was a private consolation with his own philosophy (personified as a woman) while Botton offers up his philosopher's thoughts for a public audience for his reader's consolation. I am no judge on which was the more effective work as I am yet to read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31837.The_Consolation_of_Philosophy" rel="nofollow noopener">the original work</a>.<br /><br />As for the book itself, Botton tries hard to make it entertaining and relevant and uses almost a bullet-point like efficiency to ensure that he can pack everything into an 'airport size' book.<br /><br />The framework of the book is to use the wisdom of six philosophers, almost in chronological order, to offer consolations for some of the common maladies that afflict the average person. The fact that he spends more time and pages detailing out the lives of the philosophers should not deter from the fact that he does manage to stick admirably to the overall structure of the book and does offer a coherent sequence and logic of 'consolations'.<br /><br /><br /><u>
  <b>Consolation For Unpopularity</b>
</u><br /><br />Botton uses the example and philosophy of Socrates and his life to illustrate that the judgement of others should have no real bearing on how we judge ourselves. This is not to say that we should count ourselves superior by being in the minority. No, the real message is that the weight of numbers supporting any argument or moral standpoint has nothing to do with the real strength of that position. Only reason should guide us in our judgements of ourselves and of others. In the hatred unfairly directed towards an innocent philosopher we recognize an echo of the hurt we ourselves encounter at the hands of those who are either unable or unwilling to do us justice. But if your reason tells us we are right, we should stick to our beliefs and we might be redeemed as Socrates was by the very people who condemned him and be consoled by the prospect of posterity's verdict.<br /><br /><br /><u>
  <b>Consolation For Not Having Enough Money</b>
</u><br /><br />What is wealth? Is it mere material wealth or is it anything that provides us real happiness? These were the questions that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/114041.Epicurus" title="Epicurus" rel="nofollow noopener">Epicurus</a> grappled with. His answer was that just as we are not capable of judging what is good for our physical body and would gladly gorge ourselves with unhealthy food to the point of death (as a lot of us do). so we are not capable of judging what is truly good for our souls, for our life.<br /><br />I want to belabor this point - If left to ourselves and our instinctive tastes, we would find no reason to refrain from consuming as much as we can of everything that tastes good and this only leads to a decay in bodily health. It takes an expert opinion and self-control to be able to give up this unhealthy habit and adopt a moderate and healthy diet that allows us much better health.<br /><br />Epicurus says that we similarly gorge on money and all the other pleasurable thing sin life and jump head long into the rat race thinking that is important. But only deep reflection can show us that it is a bad for our spiritual well-being and health as all that good food is for our bodily health. <br /><br />So he says pleasure is the ultimate goal of life - but what gives you true pleasure can only be found by deep reflection. So what should we dedicate all our energies to if we want a happy life?<br /><br />We should find <b>Friendship</b>, good companionship - association with people who recognize our true nature with all our defects is what we really need. in fact all our mad scramble after money and power is just a manifestation of our need to be esteemed and listened to by our fellow beings. We may seek a fortune for no reason but to secure the respect and attention of people who would otherwise look straight through us. But do we need money to get them to respect us? Would not a true friend value every word of yours and respect you even if you were penniless? <br /><br />The second most important constituent of happiness is <b>Freedom</b> - the freedom to be ourselves. This eventually connects back to being with people who will accept us as us. Epicurus and his friends made a radical innovation. In order not to have to work for people they didn't like and answer to potentially humiliating whims, they removed themselves from employment in the commercial world of Athens ('We must free ourselves from the prison of everyday affairs and polities'), and began what could best have been described as a commune, accepting a simpler way of life in exchange for independence. They would have less money but would never again have to follow the commands of odious superiors.<br /><br />Simplicity did not affect the friends' sense of status because, by distancing themselves from the values of Athens, they had ceased to judge themselves on a material basis. There was no need to be embarrassed by bare walls, and no benefit in showing off gold. Among a group of friends living outside the political and economic centre of the city, there was - in the financial sense - nothing to prove.<br /><br /><b><u>So, Happiness, an acquisition list:</u><br />1. A hut 2. Friends 3. To avoid superiors, patronization, infighting and competition 4. Thought 5. Art.</b><br /><br /><i>Happiness may indeed be difficult to attain. The obstacles are not primarily financial.</i><br /><br /><br /><u>
  <b>Consolation For Frustration</b>
</u><br /><br />All frustration arises from a faulty view of the world says <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4918776.Seneca" title="Seneca" rel="nofollow noopener">Lucius Annaeus Seneca</a>. We are frustrated because we expect the world to behave in a particular way and then reality turns out to be different. the Great Stoic philosopher advises us to be always aware that the worst scenario is always possible and to be prepared for it so that when it does happen we are ready for it and will not descend into anger, shock, anxiety or despair, all of which are marks of an unprepared mind that was not in tune with reality. Correct your worldview to accept the fact that reality is cruel and thus find escape from these common frustrations. This does not mean that you should accept everything, you may struggle mightily to avoid the misfortune but you just need to be aware of its possibility to be not prey to anger, grief and other frustrations.<br /><br /><br /><u>
  <b>Consolation For Inadequacy</b>
</u><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17241.Michel_de_Montaigne" title="Michel de Montaigne" rel="nofollow noopener">Michel de Montaigne</a> consoles us about the ultimately human nature of us all. We have to accept that we are not perfect, no one ever was. Once we accept that every inadequacy we find so appalling in ourselves is shared by millions and is one of the side effects of being human and being alive, we will learn to be less embarrassed by them and can live a more fulfilling life.<br /><br /><br /><u>
  <b>Consolation For Heartbreak </b>
</u><br /><br />The nerve to invoke the greatest pessimist of the western world to console heartbroken young Werthers!<br /><br />But it is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11682.Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer" rel="nofollow noopener">Arthur Schopenhauer</a> who is being called upon to give advice on how to deal with rejection and broken love affairs. Schopenhauer's famous 'will to life' theory which modern readers might as well read as a sort of natural selection through conditioned unconscious eugenics states that we are controlled in who we find attractive and lovable by a great force of nature which is concerned only with the need to propagate the species. It is not concerned with our happiness and more often than not we will end up with people who are our anti-thesis and inconducive to our happiness. So a happy marriage is a statistical anomaly and unnatural rather than something we can naturally expect. <br /><br />So, if and when you find yourself a Young Werther with a broken heart or a girl for that matter, understand that it is not you who were rejected but it was just that the union was not approved by the good of the species by the 'will to power' or natural selection. This might sound like an artificial explanation but think about it, please, it is all just genetics.<br /><br /><br /><u>
  <b>Consolation For Difficulties </b>
</u><br /><br />Finally <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1938.Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche" rel="nofollow noopener">Friedrich Nietzsche</a> makes his grand entry and gives <u>The Ultimate Consolation - You do not need consolations in life!</u> <br /><br />All life's difficulties are to be embraced. So accept your unpopularity, poverty, inadequacy, frustrations, heart-breaks and every sorrow as necessary to become the best you can be. If you do not have these difficulties you will be a mindless creature without knowledge of life. Use all this grief and the ills of life to forge a character and life that is noble and grand. <br /><br /><b>Your greatest gifts are your difficulties.</b><br /><br /><b>Disclaimer:</b> I have modified the views expressed in this review from that in the book to match my own understanding of these philosophers at times (especially for Nietzsche). At other times I have reproduced the core message of the book without modification. I have not distinguished the two as the original works of all these great minds are always available to anyone who finds any disparity between this review and their own convictions. I have done justice neither to this book nor to the philosophers in this review and would ask you to pursue them further if you find it interesting. I will try to do a comprehensive review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16184.Will_Durant" title="Will Durant" rel="nofollow noopener">Will Durant</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31795.The_Story_of_Philosophy" title="The Story of Philosophy by Will Durant" rel="nofollow noopener">The Story of Philosophy</a> as a counterpoint to this simplistic interpretation of multi-faceted thoughts.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6765138731382794253" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer8857714" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17983186940607072282">Now, you may laugh, but I thought this was one of the best philosophy-related books I've ever read. I recognize that it's pretty basic, a little silly, etc., but for some reason it did it for me. It's short and sweet and it humanizes otherwise esoteric knowledge.<br/><br/>After I blazed through this book one evening, I went on to read the actual writings of Epicurus, Nietzsche, and Montaigne. It was the spark I needed to get me reading philosophy again.<br/><br/>I heartily recommend it. :-)</span>

<div id="review_7657448" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7657448" />
    <a title="Joe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/400997-joe"><img alt="Joe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1258989928p2/400997.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7657448?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe" href="/user/show/400997-joe">Joe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Absolutely everyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7657448" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16106267991506717251">I have read quite a few of the original works from all the philosophers covered in this book; this nails the relevent ideas and is waaaaaay easier to read. This book could and should replace the entire pop psychology and self help sections of any bookstore. Botton reveals the truth that most of the ideas authors of those genres have been slinging around and re-using for so long were actually written a long long time ago. His format works best here and shows us that philosophy is not some far off</span>
  <span id="freeText16106267991506717251" style="display:none">I have read quite a few of the original works from all the philosophers covered in this book; this nails the relevent ideas and is waaaaaay easier to read. This book could and should replace the entire pop psychology and self help sections of any bookstore. Botton reveals the truth that most of the ideas authors of those genres have been slinging around and re-using for so long were actually written a long long time ago. His format works best here and shows us that philosophy is not some far off subject that has no relevency to our lives. It is our lives. He reveals that these philosophers weren't the strong jawed leaders we see portrayed in mable, rather they were mostly sickly depressed outcasts with no money who were just trying to figure out why things are they way they are and we can learn a lot from them. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16106267991506717251" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Antigone" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22009196-antigone"><img alt="Antigone" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1383249021p2/22009196.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2248335830?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antigone" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antigone" href="/user/show/22009196-antigone">Antigone</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22009196-antigone?shelf=philosophy-psychology">philosophy-psychology</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2248335830" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11528474451714470964">Alain de Botton is on a quest for perspective.<br/><br/>His first outing, <i>How Proust Can Change Your Life</i>, brought that legendary figure front and center in an engaging study of the Proustian approach to the universe - with special attention paid to insights useful enough to assist us in navigating the world today. Having tested those waters and determined them sufficiently warm, de Botton has moved on from Marcel to the far more ambitious (and dustier) sextet of Socrates, Epicurus, Seneca, Montaigne, Sch</span>
  <span id="freeText11528474451714470964" style="display:none">Alain de Botton is on a quest for perspective.<br /><br />His first outing, <i>How Proust Can Change Your Life</i>, brought that legendary figure front and center in an engaging study of the Proustian approach to the universe - with special attention paid to insights useful enough to assist us in navigating the world today. Having tested those waters and determined them sufficiently warm, de Botton has moved on from Marcel to the far more ambitious (and dustier) sextet of Socrates, Epicurus, Seneca, Montaigne, Schopenhauer and Nietzsche...all in an apparent search for consolation.<br /><br />Pause for amusement. (That changing your life via Proust can result in the need for consolation seems worth a wry little grin.)<br /><br />While I don't put much stock in de Botton's advice on the self-help front, the good news is that's rarely more than five percent of the content of these books. The other ninety-five invariably contains the nifty reconstitution of a prominent personality in all its baseline complexity - talents, accomplishments, fallibilities, habitual tics (the shrewish wife, the impotence, the succession of poodles). The six philosophers featured here are brought back to life in a genuinely personable way, and this makes them interesting. More than that, it makes them human. And that makes de Botton's modest volume an excellent spot to enter into the field we work so hard to keep on avoiding.<br /><br />(I mean, c'mon. You've got Plato in one hand and <i>anything else</i> in the other. You tell me which wins.)<br /><br />This was pleasant. And painless. If you're of a mind? Dig in.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11528474451714470964" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="WarpDrive" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16160897-warpdrive"><img alt="WarpDrive" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605312701p2/16160897.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1295331752?book_show_action=true">May 31, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WarpDrive" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WarpDrive" href="/user/show/16160897-warpdrive">WarpDrive</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16160897-warpdrive?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1295331752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15499347230020298808">This is a self-help book providing a very simplified, popularized version of the approach to the problems of human life as expressed by some major philosopher such as Socrates, Seneca, Epicurus, Montaigne, Schopenhauer, Nietzsche.<br/>I have mixed feelings about this book: while the author is undoubtedly quite good at providing a very readable, witty and fluent narrative, on the other hand it must be said that there can be a blurred boundary between oversimplified interpretations and unoriginal plati</span>
  <span id="freeText15499347230020298808" style="display:none">This is a self-help book providing a very simplified, popularized version of the approach to the problems of human life as expressed by some major philosopher such as Socrates, Seneca, Epicurus, Montaigne, Schopenhauer, Nietzsche.<br />I have mixed feelings about this book: while the author is undoubtedly quite good at providing a very readable, witty and fluent narrative, on the other hand it must be said that there can be a blurred boundary between oversimplified interpretations and unoriginal platitudes on one side, and a concise, simplified but still meaningful and faithful representation on the other side: the author does not always manage to strike a proper balance, and to consistently stay on the right side of such boundary. <br />In particular, I have been quite disappointed by how the philosophical thought of thinkers such as Schopenhauer has been trivialized up to the point of making it difficult to recognize. I also found the choice of Montaigne quite strange - I recognize that such choices are always quite subjective, but I can easily think of dozens of philosophers who would have been much more highly deserving of pride of place in this book. <br />On a more positive side, the representation of stoic thinking is quite good, and the book is peppered with some good points and interesting remarks and perspectives. So it is not uniformly bad at all, just uneven in quality and depth.<br /><br />Not too bad at all for a beginner with no or little previous exposure to philosophy; nice, quick, breezy and pleasant beachside reading for everybody else; just be aware that there is more consolation than actual philosophy here. <br /><br />2.5 stars - rounded up to 3, by taking into account the always commendable effort by the author to widen the audience of the subject of philosophy. Not a bad book overall.<br /> <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="15499347230020298808" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_52447979" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/52447979" />
    <a title="Gordon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1725471-gordon"><img alt="Gordon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1249603193p2/1725471.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/52447979?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gordon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gordon" href="/user/show/1725471-gordon">Gordon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer52447979" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13492811794199737993">Alain de Botton is a kind of Everyman’s philosopher – which is exactly how I like my philosophers. (In fact, he teaches philosophy at the University of London). The book is a quick romp through a half-dozen philosophers, from Socrates to Nietzsche, by applying their philosophies to the author’s everyday problems. For example, he explains Epicurus’ thought in relation to his own issues about wanting material things. Much to my surprise, Epicurus turns out to have little to do with the hedonism I </span>
  <span id="freeText13492811794199737993" style="display:none">Alain de Botton is a kind of Everyman’s philosopher – which is exactly how I like my philosophers. (In fact, he teaches philosophy at the University of London). The book is a quick romp through a half-dozen philosophers, from Socrates to Nietzsche, by applying their philosophies to the author’s everyday problems. For example, he explains Epicurus’ thought in relation to his own issues about wanting material things. Much to my surprise, Epicurus turns out to have little to do with the hedonism I always thought was the basic idea behind his beliefs. He did think that “Pleasure is the beginning and goal of a happy life”, but his pleasures turn out to be of the simple kind, not the kind that require 8-course meals. There were three kinds of sources of pleasure for Epicurus which he thought created the foundation of a happy life: <br />1)	Friendship: “Of all the things that wisdom provides to help one live one’s entire life in happiness, the greatest by far is the possession of friendship”.<br />2)	Freedom: Live a simple life, and you will free yourself of many of the burdens of work and of lusting after material goods. <br />3)	Thought: Since Epicurus is supposed to have written 300 books (almost all of which have been lost), it seems he took thinking very seriously – and also believed that writing was a great aid to thought. <br /><br />So, if you’re like me and have pretty much forgotten everything you ever learned in that Philosophy 101 course, I definitely recommend this book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13492811794199737993" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText14901539145671220441" style="display:none">Arthur Schopenhauer once said "When some political or ecclesiastical pamphlet, or novel, or poem is making a great commotion, you should remember that he who writes for fools always finds a large public. <div id="review_41194289" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41194289" />
    <a title="Aaron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1115629-aaron"><img alt="Aaron" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1209137529p2/1115629.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41194289?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron" href="/user/show/1115629-aaron">Aaron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140276619" href="/book/show/23423.The_Consolations_of_Philosophy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1115629-aaron?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41194289" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15804778021770343378">Concise, relevant, down to earth, pragmatic.<br/><br/>The spin of the book is that philosophy can help you overcome obstacles in your life (unpopularity, poverty, frustration and a broken heart among others). It's a cute angle, but not to be taken too seriously.<br/><br/>This book is a refresher, a booster injection to remind you of the contributions put forward by a handful of thinkers. A wholesome tidbit before tackling Sophie's World or similar.<br/><br/>This shot contains: Socrates, Epicurus, Seneca, Montaigne, Schopenha</span>
  <span id="freeText15804778021770343378" style="display:none">Concise, relevant, down to earth, pragmatic.<br /><br />The spin of the book is that philosophy can help you overcome obstacles in your life (unpopularity, poverty, frustration and a broken heart among others). It's a cute angle, but not to be taken too seriously.<br /><br />This book is a refresher, a booster injection to remind you of the contributions put forward by a handful of thinkers. A wholesome tidbit before tackling Sophie's World or similar.<br /><br />This shot contains: Socrates, Epicurus, Seneca, Montaigne, Schopenhauer, Nietzsche. Do not drive or operate heavy machinery while reading this book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15804778021770343378" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Todd N" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/267189-todd-n"><img alt="Todd N" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453488513p2/267189.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33608805?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Todd N" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Todd N" href="/user/show/267189-todd-n">Todd N</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33608805" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14511503851721886694">This is a strange and enjoyable mixture of philosophy and self-help (and potty words!), divided neatly into six philosophers and six vexing problems. They can be read in any order. I couldn't wait for Nietzsche (the last section) so I skipped ahead and read that fourth instead of sixth.<br/><br/>Here are the six philosophers and the corresponding issues:<br/> Socrates => Unpopularity<br/> Epicurus => Material Want<br/> Seneca => Anger<br/> Montaigne => Feelings of Inadequacy<br/> Schopenhauer => Heartache<br/> Nietzsche => Frustration</span>
  <span id="freeText14511503851721886694" style="display:none">This is a strange and enjoyable mixture of philosophy and self-help (and potty words!), divided neatly into six philosophers and six vexing problems. They can be read in any order. I couldn't wait for Nietzsche (the last section) so I skipped ahead and read that fourth instead of sixth.<br /><br />Here are the six philosophers and the corresponding issues:<br /> Socrates =&gt; Unpopularity<br /> Epicurus =&gt; Material Want<br /> Seneca =&gt; Anger<br /> Montaigne =&gt; Feelings of Inadequacy<br /> Schopenhauer =&gt; Heartache<br /> Nietzsche =&gt; Frustration<br /><br />Maybe it's my inner dilettante but I love reading popularizations -- of science, history, and now I have an author I can trust on philosophy.<br /><br />But be careful not to dismiss this book because it makes its points clearly (and with pictures to aid retention). Several times I had to force myself to slow down as I was reading it, so that I could reflect on what I was reading. The simple prose belies the heady concepts that it is covering.<br /><br />Highly recommended. If you come across this in a bookstore or library, just read the section on love (Schopenhauer) for yuks and the ultimately depressing thesis. Not having experienced heartbreak in a while (the will-to-life has already had its evil way with me), I can't vouch for what consolation (if any) the concepts in this section are.</span>

<div id="review_688774179" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/688774179" />
    <a title="David Sarkies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies"><img alt="David Sarkies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470027282p2/5856247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/688774179?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sarkies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sarkies" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies">David Sarkies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141038377" href="/book/show/5272805-the-consolations-of-philosophy">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Christians to get an understanding of how philosophy works</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to David by: </span>
      A friend from work who bought this book for me as a Kris Kringle
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer688774179" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1481747870882821863"><b>Philosophers show us how to handle life</b><br/>11 August 2013<br/><br/>	I guess the question that has been raised a number of times is whether this book is a self-help book. I guess the problem with self-help books is that people don't like to be seen reading them because it creates the suggestion that maybe they have problems and that their life is not where they want it to be – in a sense it is a neon sign that blares to the world 'I am weak and helpless'. This is probably why people don't like to admit that th</span>
  <span id="freeText1481747870882821863" style="display:none"><b>Philosophers show us how to handle life</b><br>11 August 2013<br><br>	I guess the question that has been raised a number of times is whether this book is a self-help book. I guess the problem with self-help books is that people don't like to be seen reading them because it creates the suggestion that maybe they have problems and that their life is not where they want it to be – in a sense it is a neon sign that blares to the world 'I am weak and helpless'. This is probably why people don't like to admit that they are going to psychologists because of the same thing – it makes them appear weak and socially inept. Well, to be honest with you, one of the biggest selling books ever compiled is basically a self-help book, and that would be the Bible, though I would probably suggest that it is a little more than a self-help book in that it is not so much telling us how to overcome our problems, but rather that the God who is there is willing to provide us with that assistance.<br>	Anyway, with regards to this book, it seems to take the name of a book written back in the middle ages by a guy name Boetheus. Now, I have never read the original book but it appears that Botton borrowed his ideas for this book from the original, in that the works of numerous eminent philosophers can assist us in the struggles that life throws at us. The difference is that Botton is able to draw on both modern and ancient philosophers in his search for comfort, as well as instruct us how their teachings can help us make sense of a chaotic world.<br>	What I found interesting in this book is Botton's use of pictures, and he uses them extensively in this work, no doubt providing a break in the text. This reminds of some Goodreads commentators who also extensively use pictures to attempt to outline their views on a particular work. For instances, Botton will be writing about Socrates and be discussing a painting portraying his death, and will reproduce the painting as such:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380432198i/867977._SX540_.jpg" width="381" height="311" alt="Joseph de Saint-Quentin – The Death of Socrates" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>	However, he does a little more than simply produce copies of pictures that he is referring to, or even pictures (or carvings) or the specific philosophers. If, say, he is talking about a goat (which he does) he will then produce a picture as such:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380432198i/867978.jpg" alt="Goat" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>	Or when he is talking about Neiztsche and the concept of the superman, he points out that it is not so much Neiztsche's writings that influenced Hitler, but rather his sister, as demonstrated by this picture:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380432198i/867979.jpg" alt="Elizabeth Forster-Nietzsche and Adolf Hitler" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>	Anyway, I am probably getting a bit off track by using pictures, not that using pictures is a bad thing, but I will move on and outline each of the philosophers that Botton looks at, say a few words about their consolations, and then point out that the Bible actually also has answers for these problems as well (though the philosophers are also quite helpful in that they tend to flesh these ideas out a bit more). However, one point I need to make is that after reading this book I have a little more understanding of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7491463.Francis_August_Schaeffer" title="Francis August Schaeffer" rel="nofollow noopener">Francis Schaeffer</a>'s concerns when he was talking about how modern philosophy is attempting to find away to provide answers to these problems by moving away from the bible.<br>	<b>Socrates:</b> The whole idea about Socrates is not so much his philosophy, though he does explore his philosophy and the concept of virtue, but rather the fact that at the end of his life Socrates was put on trial and sentenced to death on what turned out to be trumped up charges. However, Socrates never despaired at his sudden unpopularity because even though his philosophy may have offended people, he still believed that he was right. To back this up, Botton then outlines that after his execution, the original people that brought him to court were suddenly turned upon and either forced into exile or executed themselves. While that may be cold comfort, one must remember that it is not the man who has lived on, but his teaching. Despite that moment of unpopularity, Socrates has gone on, after death, to become an incredibly influencial philosopher.<br>	However, one should also remember that this was the same fate that Jesus suffered. There is a difference because at the end of Socrates' life he still had a core group of friends that supported him, and that when he was found guilty it was only by a small margin. Right up until the end he had friends and companions. Jesus did not. He died alone, on the cross, with pretty much everybody either deserting him or turning against him. Okay, I may be slightly exaggerating, but Socrates had more company at his death than did Jesus, yet Jesus went on to become one of the most influential figures in the history of humanity.<br>	<b>Epicurus:</b> I once suggested that the core of Epicurean philosophy is 'if it feels good, do it.' Well, after reading the section on Epicurus I have come to realise that I am pretty much correct. However, the idea that comes out here is that wealth does not necessarily bring happiness, friendship does. Okay, Epicurus did not live a life of poverty, but his philosophical writings pointed out that happiness does not come out of possessions, but out of the company of others. I can attest to that personally because even though I lived in a big house and had a nice car I also desired companionship, and love, and the lack of that still made me depressed. Also, while the car itself started off nice it pretty much ended up where all cars finish their lives: the scrap heap.<br>	However, while Epicurus is correct in pointing out that friendship is more valuable than wealth, what happens when these friendships break down. The problem with friendship is that we desire it so much that when we get caught up with the wrong type of friends we simply do not want to give them up for fear of being alone. The same is the case with people who live in abusive relationships. Also friendships are not necessarily permanent as we humans are a flawed lot and are prone to fight. While it might be fun living in a commune for a while, that is not necessarily going to last.<br>	Once again the Bible offers an answer to that as well and that is the suggestion that God is the perfect friend, and father. Granted, he may be slow at answering prayers, and may impose certain rules on us that we may not like, but seriously, is a good Earthly father going to give us everything we want. If we want to stick our tongue in a power socket, is a good father going to let us do that, or is he going to want to prevent it. You see, the difference between friends and God is that God is reliable, though the other difference is that he is not present physically, which ends up requiring a leap of faith.<br>	<i>Seneca:</i> The section on Seneca deals with frustrations, and what better person to turn to since he was the tutor to one of the most sadistic emperors Rome had ever known, and no matter how much he tried to get away, Nero would step in and prevent him. In a way, just like a spoilt child, Nero wanted Seneca as his play thing, and when he no longer wanted him, he had him killed. The basis of Seneca's philosophy is simply this: the world is a chaotic mess – learn to deal with it. To go further: just because something annoys you, it does not necessarily mean that the person causing that annoyance is specifically targeting you (though some people are sadistic enough that they will go out of their way to annoy as many people as possible). So, the crux of the philosophy is this: don't take everything so personally, expect the unexpected, and expect the worst. Goblets get smashed at parties and builders make noise – that does not mean that the builder, or the universe, is out to get you.<br>	Jesus suggested something similar, though I think the Church does not necessarily annunciate it appropriately. Basically Jesus once said 'if the world hates you, it is because they hated me first'. What he is saying is that if, as a Christian, you are being persecuted, it is not because they are persecuting you, but because they hate the Christian message. However, we can take this further. If somebody is acting like a git, and you are suffering because they are acting like a git, it does not necessarily mean that they are personally targeting you (though sometimes they are) but rather because they are basically a git. In fact, you may be surprised to discover that if you are being annoyed by a particular person, there are probably quite a lot of other people out there that are annoyed by this particular person as well, so, as Seneca says, don't take things so personally.<br>	<b>Montaigne:</b> I have written a lot about Montaigne elsewhere, but the crux of this section deals with difference. Basically, the idea is that if you are different, then that is not necessarily a bad thing because there are a lot of different people out there, and you are one of them. In fact, the Bible actually points out that each and every one of us are unique, and that is something to be joyful about.	<br>	<b>Schopenhauer:</b> Now the idea that comes out of Schopenhauer is how to deal with rejection. Basically his idea is that there is this subconscious 'will to live' namely an innate part of the human race that wants to propagate itself. However he goes further to suggest that we want to propagate with the person who will produce for us the best child, and as such we will seek out that partner that can give us that child. Therefore, if we are rejected it is not something that is conscious, but something that is subconscious. Darwin explores this idea further in his writings on sexual selection, and no doubt he borrowed from Schopenhauer's thesis when he was writing this.<br>	My concern here is that because we live in a flawed universe, this 'will to live' in itself is going to be flawed as well. Humanity does not necessarily want to breed out the worst traits and keep the best traits, because if that were the case, we would be clearly on our way to becoming <i>ubermensch</i>. However, we are not. We are still greedy, selfish, and racist. While our technology has increased, we have also remained lazy. Instead of using slaves to do the work we don't want to do we use machines. This demonstrates that this so called 'will to live' is not breeding out those traits that we don't like. In fact, the whole Hollywood premise is that we should marry physical beauty, and if the idea of the 'will to live' is to propagate beauty, then we are far from moving towards the idea of the <i>ubermensch</i>.<br>	<b>Nietzsche:</b> I cannot begin writing about Nietzsche without doing this:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380432198i/867980.jpg" width="160" height="201" alt="Frederik Nietzsche" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>	Okay, he is not necessarily one of my favourite philosophers, but I also think he is a much misunderstood philosopher. For instance, the idea of the <i>ubermensch</i> was not Hitler's idea of the Ayrian Master Race, but rather the idea of the enlightened person. A person who has risen above their base, animalistic urges, to embrace the reality of the universe, and developed the ability to think and reason. Now, the philosophy of Nietzsche that is explored here is an idea that is actually not new, and that is 'no pain, no gain'. The idea of climbing a mountain is used. To get to the top of the mountain, one must climb the mountain, and in climbing the mountain, one suffers pain, but at the top of the mountain the person is rewarded with spectacular views. Yes, granted, this is simplistic because these days we can also catch a cable car, but that can provide other forms of pain (such as waiting in line and paying exorbitant fees).<br>	Now, Nietzsche hated alcohol because he believed that it was a quick way to happiness which was not happiness in itself. Basically Nietzsche suggested that to achieve our goals, one had to experience pain to get there and all alcohol did was to dull that pain. However, he also hated the New Testament, believing that it taught the same thing. Personally I believe that the apostle Paul would disagree with him vehemently. In the end though, Nietzsche died a mad man in an insane asylum, having sold little of his philosophy, though one could argue that the pain that he had to suffer to become famous was death because Nietzsche these days is actually a well known and well read philosopher. Therefore, in conclusion, some of us may not even get to see our fame in our lifetime, yet it does not mean that we will not, one day, become famous.	</span>
  <a data-text-id="1481747870882821863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1378524026" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1378524026" />
    <a title="Mehwish Mughal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24694373-mehwish-mughal"><img alt="Mehwish Mughal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1468484909p2/24694373.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1378524026?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mehwish Mughal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mehwish Mughal" href="/user/show/24694373-mehwish-mughal">Mehwish Mughal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141038377" href="/book/show/5272805-the-consolations-of-philosophy">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mehwish by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/4777656-mjaballah">Mjaballah</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24694373-mehwish-mughal?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24694373-mehwish-mughal?shelf=recommendations">recommendations</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24694373-mehwish-mughal?shelf=my-own-my-precious">my-own-my-precious</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1378524026" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16228007119853294586"><i> The Background story </i><br/><br/>About 2 years ago, I was having a conversation with a friend about the whys and hows of life. He suggested that I read <b> The Consolations of Philosophy </b>.<br/>Back then I was only interested in hard sciences and never even thought in my wildest dreams that I would be reading philosophy and 2 years later switch my education from Science to Arts. <br/><br/>So, when I read the book I was in complete fascination with the philosophers and their philosophies. I went further and read extensively o</span>
  <span id="freeText16228007119853294586" style="display:none"><i> The Background story </i><br /><br />About 2 years ago, I was having a conversation with a friend about the whys and hows of life. He suggested that I read <b> The Consolations of Philosophy </b>.<br />Back then I was only interested in hard sciences and never even thought in my wildest dreams that I would be reading philosophy and 2 years later switch my education from Science to Arts. <br /><br />So, when I read the book I was in complete fascination with the philosophers and their philosophies. I went further and read extensively on Socrates, Epicurus and Nietzsche. Socrates became my hero. As days rolled into months, and months into years, more books were read and this one became a part of my subconscious helping me make the tough decisions in life, cementing my personality and ideologies without ever surfacing in my conscious mind. I constantly suggested this book to my reader friends who stay clear of the path of philosophy regarding it as something that messes with your brain. Now, I do not know whether that claim is real or not because of course I have no idea whether my brain is deluded or functional. <br /><br />2 years later, my sister finished the book (after a lot of coaxing and pressure) and returned it back. I had a very curious feeling about this book which apparently turned my life around 180 degrees and I decided to read again to see whether I feel the same way that I felt back then or have I grown out of it. And oh yes! All those memories came floating back.<br /><br /><i> The actual review </i><br /><br />The book is divided into six sections. <br />1- On unpopularity (Socrates)<br />2- Not having enough money (Epicurus)<br />3- Frustration (Seneca)<br />4- Inadequacy (Montaigne)<br />5- A broken heart (Schopenhauer)<br />6- Difficulties (Nietzsche)<br /><br />The writing style is accessible. The philosophers are simplified but then again that was the very reason I could get through the book without feeling overwhelmed. The philosophies are plucked from the greatest books and molded into a form that is relevant to the reader. <br /><br />As I read through it again I used a different set of eyes, I was not so much influenced by the philosophies (as I had been earlier) but by the philosophers and their individuality, their place and time in history, their influences and their thoughts. It was as if I could see right through. I felt much closer and nostalgic. <br /> <br />I critically analysed my life through this book after finishing every part and proudly came to a conclusion that I have taken a lot of things on board since the last time I read it. <br /><br />Will I read it again? Yes, if life permits, I will, in another 5 years.<br /><br /><br />I recommend it to all!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16228007119853294586" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

The author will find out the answers for your common problems, and dish them out. On top of that, his answers are not his own. He just plucked out six great thinke</span>
  <span id="freeText16703650827195863913" style="display:none">Absolute, complete, TRASH! <br /><br />I have ranted extensively about this book at <a href="http://www.solomonsays.in/items/13" rel="nofollow noopener">SolomonSays</a>, but still not over it - so here goes again.<br /><br />This is not a philosophy book. The discipline of philosophy is the discipline of asking questions and finding out answers. On the contrary, the formula of The Consolations of philosophy is quite the opposite. The author will find out the answers for your common problems, and dish them out. On top of that, his answers are not his own. He just plucked out six great thinkers, dumbed down their ideas, and had them published.<br /><br />It's like going to a doctor who prescribes only generic drugs, and that too after asking his old uncle who used to be a great doctor. <br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1433774230" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1433774230" />
    <a title="Suha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33599214-suha"><img alt="Suha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407013913p2/33599214.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1433774230?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Suha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Suha" href="/user/show/33599214-suha">Suha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1433774230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8006189710203927223">I am wondering if I should consider this book a self-help book with a philosophical aspect in which the author attempts to address life’s difficulties through a trip with the well-known philosophers.<br/> For instance, he starts with Socrates and the consolation for unpopularity: the author sheds the light on the fact that we tend to associate what is popular with what is right. “We may be silenced by impressive figures who tell us forcefully that money is essential to virtue”. Moreover, he quotes So</span>
  <span id="freeText8006189710203927223" style="display:none">I am wondering if I should consider this book a self-help book with a philosophical aspect in which the author attempts to address life’s difficulties through a trip with the well-known philosophers.<br /> For instance, he starts with Socrates and the consolation for unpopularity: the author sheds the light on the fact that we tend to associate what is popular with what is right. “We may be silenced by impressive figures who tell us forcefully that money is essential to virtue”. Moreover, he quotes Socrates: “Are you not ashamed that you give your attention to acquiring as much money as possible, and similarly with reputation and honor, and give no attention or thought to truth and understanding and the perfection of your soul”. As a result, we should be judged by our logic of justice and injustice not as to conformity with the majority.<br />De Botton consoles us with not having enough money through the philosophy of Epicurus and how lived his life first seeking pleasures of food and sex claiming that he had to vomit twice a day because he ate so much. Throughout his quest of happiness, he found the following building blocks: Friendship, freedom, and thought as the necessary musts for happiness which in fact are not related to money. <br />As for frustration, the consolation comes from Seneca, and he quotes “we are mistaken if we believe any part of the world is exempt and safe”; so bad things will surely occur, but they won’t be as bad as we fear and our only way to survive the frustration is to accept that it is only a normal aspect of life. <br />Montaigne was the philosopher chosen for the consolation of inadequacy; Montaigne criticized the unhappy scholars who were always looking for more whereas he found the goats to be happiest saying the following “she had never read the Tusculan Disputations nor Cicero’s On the Laws, and yet she seemed content nibbling at stray pieces of lettuce…” so he questioned whether the mind gave us anything to be grateful for as it carried pain, worries, jealousy, greed and untamable appetites. The topics of sexual inadequacy, cultural inadequacy and intellectual inadequacy were addressed with simplicity and acceptance of what life offered us; striving for wisdom but not far from folly is achievement enough.<br />The broken heart was consoled by Schopenhauer by addressing the matter of love; where love’s ultimate aim is not intellectual entertainment but the procreation of children. It is solely a key to continuation of the species. As he was broken hearted many times, he claims the answers to why him or why her lies in the answer of producing healthy children and intelligent offspring; a manifestation of the will to life. <br />The last chapter tackles difficulties, and Nietzsche, throughout his life’s journey in writing discovered that the most fulfilling human projects are inseparable from a degree of torment and that the sources of our greatest joys are close to those of our greatest pains. Nietzsche wrote most of his work while being in contact in nature; in a stage of his life, he got interested in gardening and he discovered that in the roots of a plant lie anger, pity, curiosity and vanity, whereas it blossoms into art, beauty and love. In our hearts, we long for belongings, success, fame and power; but those who do not have it, claim that as per Christianity, they are indifferent to what they secretly long for; as people must face difficulties to achieve and be joyous of what they have. <br />Critique: in my opinion, this book is great for those who are not aware of the philosophies/philosophers; it is a sort of a recap; moreover, it gives great insight of consolations on the stated doors of unhappiness based on strong philosophical foundations which in my opinion is similar to what is prevalent in our days through the recent self-help books. There are some figures/ illustrations in this book, showing sometimes paintings, the philosophers themselves, some photos and a visual aid of what is stated. Easy tone, straightforward, and what I also liked is that it takes you back to a small biography of the philosophers themselves. I do recommend <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8006189710203927223" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/348752552?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Liz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Liz" href="/user/show/9857088-liz">Liz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141038377" href="/book/show/5272805-the-consolations-of-philosophy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9857088-liz?shelf=have-book">have-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9857088-liz?shelf=did-not-finish">did-not-finish</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer348752552" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5661470253914152212">Book started off strong. I was very interested in the sections about Socrates, Epicurus & Seneca. The philosophies of these guys are thousands of years old but still so relevant and applicable to life today. I was throughly enjoying how the author was telling you a little bit about the life and times of these guys and how their philosophies can give people of today consolations for their problems. Then we moved into wealthy whiny man's territory. Seriously I skimmed the whole second part of this</span>
  <span id="freeText5661470253914152212" style="display:none">Book started off strong. I was very interested in the sections about Socrates, Epicurus &amp; Seneca. The philosophies of these guys are thousands of years old but still so relevant and applicable to life today. I was throughly enjoying how the author was telling you a little bit about the life and times of these guys and how their philosophies can give people of today consolations for their problems. Then we moved into wealthy whiny man's territory. Seriously I skimmed the whole second part of this book. The last three guys; Montaigne, Schopenhauer &amp; Nietzsche, were so freaking annoying, 'oh poor me, I have so much money, but can't get it up and fresh, nubile girls half my age won't marry me' Please....old guys are just gross....deal with it! <br /><br />But seriously, maybe Botton didn't do their philosophies justice, but I don't see how a group of guys writing about their lives and thoughts that are mainly applicable to their specific lives is philosophy?? I have a niggling feeling that Botton dumb down the philosophies of these guys in order to sell a self help book using other people's ideas because I have touched on the last three guys work in my tertiary studies and they seemed deeper and less fucking whiny. Maybe Botton is the whiny inadequate bitch and that is how he interpreted their work or how he felt it was applicable to his life. I think I will do further inquiry into Mont, Scho and Nietz.....I mean how can a guy with such an awesome moustache be so damned whiny?!<br /><br />I recommend the first half of this book for everyone who wants to know a little bit of surface level philosophy, and the second half to nerdy, inadequate whiny men with small flaccid pensises that could never get a date and whose mother was too over-bearing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5661470253914152212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2382071275" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2382071275" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2382071275?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aryeh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aryeh" href="/user/show/25611710-aryeh">Aryeh</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2382071275" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3006190937345601909">I could listen to de Botton explain that there is a Russia sized meteor hurtling toward our planet and leave the conversation feeling understood and comforted. Hearing him describe his understanding of some of the greatest Western philosophers and philosophies was really quite a treat. Anecdotes and insights abound in an exploration of both the greatest and consoling ideas, as well as the minds and bodies they came from. As always , de Botton is reasonable, convincing, almost spookily in touch w</span>
  <span id="freeText3006190937345601909" style="display:none">I could listen to de Botton explain that there is a Russia sized meteor hurtling toward our planet and leave the conversation feeling understood and comforted. Hearing him describe his understanding of some of the greatest Western philosophers and philosophies was really quite a treat. Anecdotes and insights abound in an exploration of both the greatest and consoling ideas, as well as the minds and bodies they came from. As always , de Botton is reasonable, convincing, almost spookily in touch with the human condition. In the spirit of Montaigne's assertion that books are of value to the extent that they are practically helpful, I particularly enjoyed his exploration of heartbreak from the perspective of the will to life. Bleak yet consoling. <br /><br />Side note: I recently reached out to de Botton to thank him for his work, and he responded authentically and promptly. He's that kind of guy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3006190937345601909" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9969625736322655994" style="display:none">probably could've given it two stars, mostly bc I was disappointed considering how much I love Alain de Botton. "he offered scope for valuable and yet until then neglected dimensions of Montaigne's character - which suggests that we pick our friends not only because they are kind and enjoyable company, but also, perhaps more importantly, because they understand us for who we think we are."
