<div id="review_385483748" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/385483748" />
    <a title="Rhoda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5675578-rhoda"><img alt="Rhoda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1343337364p2/5675578.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/385483748?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rhoda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rhoda" href="/user/show/5675578-rhoda">Rhoda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer385483748" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9431122336544098558">This book is engaging and the writing limpid enough, but it suffers from a few major weaknesses for which I dock 3 stars. First, it is larded with highly gratuitous drawings that, on more than one occasion, made me think that the book was intended for students age 10+. Second, it occasionally suffers from slippery/specious reasoning, generalizations, and redundancies. Lastly, and this is the most egregious oversight of all, it dispenses with rigorous analysis of the ideas of the philosophers men</span>
  <span id="freeText9431122336544098558" style="display:none">This book is engaging and the writing limpid enough, but it suffers from a few major weaknesses for which I dock 3 stars. First, it is larded with highly gratuitous drawings that, on more than one occasion, made me think that the book was intended for students age 10+. Second, it occasionally suffers from slippery/specious reasoning, generalizations, and redundancies. Lastly, and this is the most egregious oversight of all, it dispenses with rigorous analysis of the ideas of the philosophers mentioned in the service of dumbing down the same for the lay reader. For instance, in the chapter on "Consolations for a Broken Heart," de Botton presents Schopenhauer's will to life in an extremely digestible format, but there's just one problem that renders the whole chapter moot: the will to life simply cannot account for the preferences of homosexuals and thus cannot be a universal truth. It's also possible to discern faint contradictions in de Botton's messages: for instance, reading his essay on Montaigne, who emerges as the most humane philosopher in his cast of characters, almost seduces the reader into living a life of Christian complacency - the very kind of lifestyle that Nietzsche vehemently repudiated, as recounted in the final chapter. It must be said, however, that de Botton deftly melds biographical facts of all the philosophers with a few aspects of their individual philosophies, and there is no shortage of interesting facts about the thoughts of six great men. Herewith, the main takeaways of my reading:<br /><br />- Socrates: a correct belief held without an awareness of how to respond rationally to objections is a true opinion; knowledge involves understanding not only why something is true, but also why its alternatives are false.<br />- for Epicurus, philosophy is meant to "help us interpret our indistinct pulses of distress and desire and save us from mistaken schemes for happiness."<br />-for Epicurus, desires are i) natural and necessary (e.g. friends, freedom, thought); ii) natural but unnecessary (e.g. house, banquets); iii) neither natural nor necessary (e.g. fame, power). Also, happiness is absence of active pain.<br />- "a well-mounted Epicurean advertising campaign would have the power to precipitate global economic collapse...levels of consumption would be destroyed by greater self-awareness and appreciation of simplicity." So much for the image of Epicurus as patron saint of hedonism!<br />- Seneca "conceived of philosophy as a discipline to assist human beings in overcoming conflicts between their wishes and reality."<br />- For Seneca, "anger results from faulty reasoning; "what makes us angry are dangerously optimistic notions about what the world and other people are like. How badly we react to frustration is critically determined by what we think of as normal. Our frustrations are tempered by what we understand we can expect from the world, by our experience of what it is normal to hope for. We will cease to be so angry once we cease to be so hopeful."<br />- Seneca also counseled, somewhat ludicrously, that we must "hold the possibility of disaster in mind at all times." He invoked goddess Fortune as a capricious adversary who "did not evaluate her victims and reward them according to merit."<br />- Stoics says the wise are identified by their equanimity in response to sudden poverty.<br />- Seneca on resignation: "An animal, struggling against the noose, tightens it...there is no yoke so tight that it will not hurt the animal less if it pulls with it than if it fights against it. The one alleviation for overwhelming evils is to endure and bow to necessity."<br />- "Montaigne elaborated upon advantages of living as an animal rather than as a reasoning (and well-read) human." This despite having a thousand volumes in his library and being conversant in the classics at an early age.<br />- Montaigne consigns to learning knowledge of grammar, Latin, Greek, logic, etc. Wisdom, in contrast, is everything that can help a person to live well (i.e. happily and morally).<br />- Schopenhauer: "To marry means to do everything possible to become an object of disgust to each other."<br />- will-to-life: the force within us which overrules reason; "an inherent drive within human beings to stay alive and reproduce." "Love is nothing but the conscious manifestation of the will-to-life's discovery of an ideal co-parent." Bleakly, "the pursuit of personal happiness and the production of healthy children are two radically contrasting projects."<br />- Nietzsche: "In the eyes of people who are seeing us for the first time usually we are nothing more than a single individual trait which leaps to the eye and determines the whole impression we make."</span>
  <a data-text-id="9431122336544098558" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1731150417" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1731150417" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Howard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5027617-jeffrey-howard"><img alt="Jeffrey Howard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552582995p2/5027617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1731150417?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Howard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Howard" href="/user/show/5027617-jeffrey-howard">Jeffrey Howard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5027617-jeffrey-howard?shelf=philosophy-general">philosophy-general</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1731150417" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4018367680998099729">Alain de Botton has become one of my favorite contemporary public intellectuals, reviving an approach to philosophy and art which has been derided by most high brow philosophers and scholars. He uses 6 important philosophical figures to make one major--and incredibly compelling--point; ideas matter only in as much as they help us to live well. He means this in the most concrete sense. If any adventure into the world of ideas, any assertion is made, which does not equip us with the tools to navig</span>
  <span id="freeText4018367680998099729" style="display:none">Alain de Botton has become one of my favorite contemporary public intellectuals, reviving an approach to philosophy and art which has been derided by most high brow philosophers and scholars. He uses 6 important philosophical figures to make one major--and incredibly compelling--point; ideas matter only in as much as they help us to live well. He means this in the most concrete sense. If any adventure into the world of ideas, any assertion is made, which does not equip us with the tools to navigate existence pains and daily challenges, then we should give it up. Cast it aside. If your philosophy does not change the way another person lives, to enliven their hearts, expand their imagination, provide them insight into their relationships with themselves and others, then it is abstraction--useless, navel-gazing, intellectual snobbery. Alain de Botton is accessible, charming, and hopeful despite his penchant for pessimism. Citing Schopenhauer, the philosopher of pessimism he refrains "there is only one inborn error, and that is the notion that we exist in order to be happy...much would have been gained if through timely advice and instruction young people could have had eradicated from their minds the erroneous notion that the world has a great deal to offer them." <br /><br />He begins the book with his weakest chapter, a celebration of Socrates and the consolation one can find in "truth" and "reason" despite holding unpopular beliefs. Sure, Socrates is important. Every person exposed to any philosophy has heard that. Socrates never really moved him in his dialogues. His inclusion of Epicurius made up for that. <br /><br />His chapters on Schopenhauer and Seneca stand as the strongest contributions, investigating, respectively, "Consolation for a Broken Heart" and "Consolation for Frustration." De Botton gently rallies against the excesses of romanticism and idealism. We have to accept our circumstances and somewhat limited ability to will a different world into existence. He reminds us that we are like dogs bound by a leash to a moving cart. The cart represents the world, a chain of events that continues to roll forward, regardless of what we do. We can fuss and fight all we want, but if we resist this unstoppable motion, we will get dragged and destroyed. Still, we have freedom enough, despite being tethered, to make choices and adjust how we respond to circumstances. Wisdom lies in knowing what we can and cannot change (and the Serenity Prayer popularly states). <br /><br />As a recovering romantic and idealist, this book was a good correction for me. Life is hard. Philosophy is good, as long as it is useful. Philosophy doesn't have to be dry, stuffy, and unaffecting! This might be a decent book for a high school or college student needing convincing of the uses of philosophy. The Consolation of Philosophy is a surprisingly light read, dressed up with stories and fun facts about 6 great thinkers. <br /><br />Philosophy is for the masses, and de Botton makes that possible. He is a regular man's philosopher: "Not everything which makes us feel better is good for us. Not everything which hurts may be bad."</span>
  <a data-text-id="4018367680998099729" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2448454082?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sippy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sippy" href="/user/show/46466326-sippy">Sippy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789025419622" href="/book/show/11745145-de-troost-van-de-filosofie">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46466326-sippy?shelf=un-barely-finished">un-barely-finished</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/46466326-sippy?shelf=filosofie">filosofie</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2448454082" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer632157775520105489">The book starts with Socrates and his death sentence, which is an interesting way to start a book on philosophy. It tells the life stories of some of the classic philosophers and in the beginning I found it quite interesting. It provided a backdrop for how some of their philosophical works could have evolved. Never liked all the pictures in the book. Some of the old paintings stay current, but with modern photos of two decades ago it makes the book feel dated. <br/><br/>After a short while I got tired of </span>
  <span id="freeText632157775520105489" style="display:none">The book starts with Socrates and his death sentence, which is an interesting way to start a book on philosophy. It tells the life stories of some of the classic philosophers and in the beginning I found it quite interesting. It provided a backdrop for how some of their philosophical works could have evolved. Never liked all the pictures in the book. Some of the old paintings stay current, but with modern photos of two decades ago it makes the book feel dated. <br /><br />After a short while I got tired of all the biographies. The book isn't so much about philosophy as it is about philosophers. I am not into autobiographies nor too much storytelling in non fiction works, so I ended up just scanning most of the book. I could have done with a much thinner and shorter book that was much more on topic. <br /><br />If you want an introduction into the lives of a few philosophers this book may be for you. If you're into philosophy itself, don't bother.<br /><br /></span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('HgvTZvZa8+V/L2tWj/mxjy0ISd0CpQfoeQ0joR3QGn4nOskl6ISK/T/PqAknbATzKKXYfSMCxGi6p1/UxFA4Qw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('qFdOrgnQgcmQipIVlxXStvFGMF0BQXS91lGs25c87T2RZlTtFw740dBqUUo/gGfK9Ouh/SDmtz0V+9CuTrzPAA==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_42615795'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" published 2018\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer17816961863774288256\">في هذا الكتاب دفاعٌ عن الحياة البسيطة، وفق بوح سبق إليه نيتشه بتواضع الكبار حين قال: [إن نمط الحياة البسيطة هدف بعيد للغاية بالنسبة لي، وسأنتظر حتى يأتي أناس أكثر حكمة مني ليعثروا لنا عليه]. فما الحياة البسيطة؟ إنها حياة أعيشها بأقل ما يمكن من الأوها<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17816961863774288256\" style=\"display:none\">في هذا الكتاب دفاعٌ عن الحياة البسيطة، وفق بوح سبق إليه نيتشه بتواضع الكبار حين قال: [إن نمط الحياة البسيطة هدف بعيد للغاية بالنسبة لي، وسأنتظر حتى يأتي أناس أكثر حكمة مني ليعثروا لنا عليه]. فما الحياة البسيطة؟ إنها حياة أعيشها بأقل ما يمكن من الأوهام، حياة أتصالح فيها مع قَدَري الخاص، ولا أقارن نفسي بأي قَدَر لأي إنسان آخر. حياة أكون فيها كما أنا لا كما يريدني الآخرون أن أكون. حياة أبسُط فيها سلطاني على نفسي، بحيث تكون انفعالاتي ومشاعري صادقة في التعبير عن القيَم التي أعبّر عنها وأدعو إليها. حياة أصنع فيها المتعة بأقل الأشياء، وأبدع السعادة والفرح بأبسط السُّبل، حيث تكون البهجة الحقيقية هي بهجة الشعور بالنمو والارتقاء. لكن العودة إلى الحياة البسيطة هي الرحلة الأصعب بعد أن ضيّعنا الطريق إليها في غمرة الأوهام الكبرى. قد يظن البعض أن من أيسر الأمور أن نعيش حياة بسيطة! هذا غير صحيح. هذا يحتاج إلى التخلّي عن الطمع في تحصيل الأشياء غير الضرورية للعيش. ويحتاج إلى قيم رفيعة تجاه الطبيعة والبشر، وتجاه النفس. هذا الكتاب قبل أن يكون موجّهٌ لكم، موجّهٌ لي، كتبته لنفسي أوّلاً. إنه محاولة لتبيان دور الفلسفة في صنع الحياة، وذلك حتى تمارس دورها الذي هو الدور الأكثر حيوية: فليس دور الفلسفة أن تمنحنا القدرة على فهم النصوص وحسب، بل He was approached by a well-dressed man who told him in a quiet tone that he was going to kill himself unless the philosopher could give him a valid reason not to. Not having the time to wax philosophic on the matter, Durant did his best to furnish the man with reasons to continue his existence. Haunted by the encounter with the despondent stranger, Durant contacted 100 luminaries in the arts, politics, religion and sciences, challenging them to respond not only to the fundamental question of life\'s meaning (in the abstract) but also to relate how they each (in the particular) found meaning, purpose and fulfillment in their own lives. Durant turned their answers and his own into a book entitled &quot;On The Meaning Of Life,&quot; which was released to the general public in 1932. Unpromoted, the litte treasure found its way into few hands, and almost no copies of the book exist today. Now available for a new generation through Promethean Press, &quot;On The Meaning Of Life&quot; is a powerful book on a very powerful topic. In this book Will Durant has fashioned an unprecedented &quot;dream team&quot; of luminaries that is both profound and diverse: poets, philosophers, saints, inmates, athletes, Nobel Prize winners, college professors, psychologists, entertainers, musicians, authors and leaders. Within their varied insights, despite their uniqueness as individuals and the very different lives they led, the reader will note a consistent thread running through their viewpoints, revealing a A<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText10863763073884697211\" style=\"display:none\">A leading German metaphysician of the 19th century, Arthur Schopenhauer (1788–1860) exerted an influence far beyond the hermetic world of philosophy, with adherents ranging from Richard Wagner and Friedrich Nietzsche to Leo Tolstoy and Thomas Mann. Among Schopenhauer\'s chief contributions to the field of philosophy are his rejection of the idealism of his contemporaries and his embrace of a practical variety of materialism. He jettisons the traditional philosophic jargon for a brisk, compelling style that employs direct terms to express the metaphysics of the will.In The Wisdom of Life, an essay from Schopenhauer\'s final work, Parerga und Paralipomena (1851), the philosopher favors individual strength of will and independent, reasoned deliberation over the tendency to act on irrational impulses. He examines the ways in which life can be arranged to derive the highest degree of pleasure and success, presents guidelines to achieving this full and rich manner of living, and advises that even a life well lived must always aspire to grander heights. Abounding in subjects of enduring relevance, Schopenhauer\'s highly readable work appears here in an excellent translation.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"10863763073884697211\" href=\"#\" <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17875246281519230142">Alain de Botton is a writer and television producer who lives in London and aims to make philosophy relevant to everyday life. He can be contacted by email directly via <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.alaindebotton.com'>www.alaindebotton.com</a><br/> <br/>He is a writer of essayistic books, which refer both to his own experiences and ideas- and those of artists, philosophers and thinkers. It's a style of writing that has been termed a 'philosophy of everyday li</span>
  <span id="freeText17875246281519230142" style="display:none">Alain de Botton is a writer and television producer who lives in London and aims to make philosophy relevant to everyday life. He can be contacted by email directly via <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.alaindebotton.com">www.alaindebotton.com</a><br /> <br />He is a writer of essayistic books, which refer both to his own experiences and ideas- and those of artists, philosophers and thinkers. It's a style of writing that has been termed a 'philosophy of everyday life.'<br /><br />His first book, Essays in Love [titled On Love in the US], minutely analysed the process of falling in and out of love. The style of the book was unusual, because it mixed elements of a novel together with reflections and analyses normally found in a piece of non-fiction. It's a book of which many readers are still fondest.<br /><br />Bibliography:<br />* Essays In Love (1993)<br />* The Romantic Movement (1994)<br />* Kiss and Tell (1995)<br />* How Proust Can Change Your Life (1997)<br />* The Consolations of Philosophy (2000)<br />* The Art of Travel (2002)<br />* Status Anxiety (2004)<br />* The Architecture of Happiness (2006)<br />* The Pleasures and Sorrows of Work (2009)</span>
  <a data-text-id="17875246281519230142" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/14280291-the-consolations-of-philosophy">Quotes from The Consolations ...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Booksellers are the most valuable destination for the lonely, given the numbers of books written because authors couldn't find anyone to talk to.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/294246-booksellers-are-the-most-valuable-destination-for-the-lonely-given">137 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;...no one is able to produce a great work of art without experience, nor achieve a worldly position immediately, nor be a great lover at the first attempt; and in the interval between initial failure and subsequent success, in the gap between who we wish one day to be and who we are at present, must come pain, anxiety, envy and humiliation. We suffer because we cannot spontaneously master the ingredients of fulfilment.&rdquo;</span>
